  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6946ed6e-03d7-45a3-877f-40fe42b7388a'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11765240006904602544">Told with P. D. James's trademark suspense, insightful characterization, and riveting storytelling, <i>The Children of Men</i> is a story of a world with no children and no future. The human race has become infertile, and the last generation to be born is now adult. Civilization itself is crumbling as suicide and despair become commonplace. Oxford historian Theodore Faron, apathe</span>
  <span id="freeText11765240006904602544" style="display:none">Told with P. D. James's trademark suspense, insightful characterization, and riveting storytelling, <i>The Children of Men</i> is a story of a world with no children and no future. The human race has become infertile, and the last generation to be born is now adult. Civilization itself is crumbling as suicide and despair become commonplace. Oxford historian Theodore Faron, apathetic toward a future without a future, spends most of his time reminiscing. Then he is approached by Julian, a bright, attractive woman who wants him to help get her an audience with his cousin, the powerful Warden of England. She and her band of unlikely revolutionaries may just awaken his desire to live . . . and they may also hold the key to survival for the human race.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12498628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17410697994430556804"><br/><b>Posted at <a href='http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/08/the-children-of-men.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br/><br/>I went to the library to spice up my life and came across a display inviting me to go on a blind date with a book. Each one was covered in brown wrapping paper with a big red heart. Underneath the heart was a very brief description. The one I picked up said “Receptive and chilling”. <br/><br/>It was fun driving home with a book I knew absolutely nothing about. I couldn’t wait to get it home, pour myself a glass of wine, strip off its cover, and learn its secrets. To my disappointm</span>
  <span id="freeText17410697994430556804" style="display:none"><br /><b>Posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2013/08/the-children-of-men.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br /><br />I went to the library to spice up my life and came across a display inviting me to go on a blind date with a book. Each one was covered in brown wrapping paper with a big red heart. Underneath the heart was a very brief description. The one I picked up said “Receptive and chilling”. <br /><br />It was fun driving home with a book I knew absolutely nothing about. I couldn’t wait to get it home, pour myself a glass of wine, strip off its cover, and learn its secrets. To my disappointment, it was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41913.The_Children_of_Men" title="The Children of Men by P.D. James" rel="nofollow noopener">The Children of Men</a>, a book I read shortly after it came out. I liked it well enough at the time, but found that years later nothing stood out for me but the Quietus and the feral Painted Faces. I saw the film around 2007 and can’t remember a single thing about it, only that there was more action and less reflection and introspection.<br /><br />In 2021, the world is ending quietly. No babies have been born since 1995, the last one killed when he was just 25. People are getting older, trapped in routines, becoming resigned. Infrastructure is falling apart from lack of maintenance and small towns are losing their population. <br /><br />Theodore Faron is a history professor who no longer has any children of his own and none to teach. He is keeping a journal to record the last half of his life and lives a solitary existence until he meets Julian and a small group of people who desire to revolt against the dictatorship of England, whose leader happens to be Theo’s cousin Xan. <br /><br />When I first read this book, I found the characters largely bland and uninteresting and much preferred the second half when Theo and the five revolutionaries were on the run.<br /><br />Now, I found I rather enjoyed reading about Theo’s childhood and relationship with Xan, his failed marriage, the people he encounters, his feelings about the events going on around him, and the gradual process of his falling in love. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“A failed marriage is the most humiliating confirmation of the transitory seduction of the flesh. Lovers can explore every line, every curve and hollow, of the beloved’s body, can together reach the height of inexpressible ecstasy; yet how little it matters when love or lust at last dies and we are left with disputed possessions, lawyers’ bills, the sad detritus of the lumber-room, when the house chosen, furnished, possessed with enthusiasm and hope has become a prison, when faces are set in lines of peevish resentment and bodies no longer desired are observed in all their imperfections with a dispassionate and disenchanted eye.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I enjoyed this book much more with the second reading. Maybe it’s because I am now Theo’s age and can understand his feelings much better. Or maybe I have more patience and prefer rich characterization and lovely descriptions of countryside to lots of mindless action. Now that the book is fresh in my mind, I’ll think about watching the film again.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17410697994430556804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28701117" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28701117?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307275431" href="/book/show/406161.The_Children_of_Men">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=made-my-eyes-bleed">made-my-eyes-bleed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28701117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16459010472067169073"><i>"Told with P. D. James's trademark suspense, insightful characterization, and riveting storytelling"...</i><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380314574i/688956.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I have wanted to read this book for a long time. I loved the movie. I thought it was brilliant, exciting, suspenseful and terrifying all at once. It was everything the book should have been... but was not. <br/><br/>What the book was, unfortunately, was big stretches of yawn interspersed by long-jumps of "Are we there yet? Are we there yet? Are we fucking there yet?" and little bunny-hops of "Oh, that's </span>
  <span id="freeText16459010472067169073" style="display:none"><i>"Told with P. D. James's trademark suspense, insightful characterization, and riveting storytelling"...</i><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380314574i/688956.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I have wanted to read this book for a long time. I loved the movie. I thought it was brilliant, exciting, suspenseful and terrifying all at once. It was everything the book should have been... but was not. <br><br>What the book was, unfortunately, was big stretches of yawn interspersed by long-jumps of "Are we there yet? Are we there yet? Are we fucking there yet?" and little bunny-hops of "Oh, that's interesting" moments. <br><br>As a dystopia, the world that James created is plausible, perhaps even likely, should the events that changed the world come about in our own reality. Mens' little swimmers forgot their floaties, and thus the race is more of a floundering then a sinking, then a dying off. No more babies. Wonder if anyone checked where the <a href="http://www.vhemt.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Voluntary Human Extinction Movement</a> people were in Omega year 1995, eh? Sprinkling a little sumthin' in the water? Hmmmm?<br><br>I digress. The world... plausible. But I just really couldn't bring myself to care. It was hard to give a crap about really anyone in this book, including humanity in general. I didn't care about the fact that the whole world was dying because I didn't give a shit that the individuals shown in the book were dying. Everyone was so despicable and shitty. Julian was maybe the only exception, but despite her desire to change things and do something better for the world, I still just didn't care. <br><br>I'm supposed to care about a world when the lens I get to see it through is so covered in shit I don't even want to stand downwind of it? A man whose only thought for his 27-years-dead toddler daughter is that she was an inconvenience anyway? Really? He can't find ONE positive thing about his own daughter in almost 30 years? <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380314574i/688957.jpg" height="180" width="180" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I <i>liked</i> Theo Faron in the movie. He was maybe selfish, maybe not the nicest guy, but he was real, and I liked him. His book character? Not so much. I found it very, very hard to even muster up a little meh for him, even when he comes around to the "good guys'" team. You know... until he takes the One Ring for himself, that is. <br><br>Ugh, and don't even get me started on Theo's diaries. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380314574i/688958.png" height="280" width="320" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>You kind of expect historians to be a little dull. Introverted, selfish asshole historians to be duller still. But wow. Seriously, fucking wow, were Theo's diary entries duller than shit. Do I need a minute by minute recap of how he spent his adolescent summers at his cousin's estate house? No. Please no. Please. Establish the history in a flashback, in a home video, in a memory, in... something, ANYTHING, other than the diary entries of man who has nothing at all better to do with the unlimited vocabulary, time and Bic pens at his disposal than write the most trivial boring bullshit diary that will never ever be read ever. Except by me. <br><br>FML. <br><br>This book gets this many <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380314574i/688959.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> stars. </span>

<div id="review_275660261" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/275660261" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/275660261?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307275431" href="/book/show/406161.The_Children_of_Men">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=20-ce">20-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=uk">uk</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer275660261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12751088809374851857">I have come to realize, years after writing this review, that is it is marked by a naïve Lamarckism--a belief in the heredity of acquired characteristics. But I'll let it stand as a reminder of my errors, and how much I have learned since then.<br/><br/>---<br/><br/>I never was much of a genre reader but at some time in my middle years I was assailed by a love of dystopias. There's nothing like a vivid tale of the world ending to truly set me at my ease. It did not occur to me until I read Norman Cohn's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Pursuit%20of%20the%20Millennium' title='The Pursuit of the Millennium' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Pursuit</a></span>
  <span id="freeText12751088809374851857" style="display:none">I have come to realize, years after writing this review, that is it is marked by a naïve Lamarckism--a belief in the heredity of acquired characteristics. But I'll let it stand as a reminder of my errors, and how much I have learned since then.<br /><br />---<br /><br />I never was much of a genre reader but at some time in my middle years I was assailed by a love of dystopias. There's nothing like a vivid tale of the world ending to truly set me at my ease. It did not occur to me until I read Norman Cohn's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Pursuit%20of%20the%20Millennium" title="The Pursuit of the Millennium" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pursuit of the Millennium</a> why dystopic narratives were so satisfying on an almost physiological level. I realized it was a hardwired need, evolved by centuries of my whack-job millenarian forebears, for apocalyptic solace. These eschatological needs are still within me and going strong. They include a desire for angels as messengers of the apocalypse, an irrational longing for the rewards of paradise, and an overwhelming desire to witness those less pure of heart than myself receive their fiery comeuppance.<br /><br />Fortunately, unlike my forebears, I have not had to run riot over the Bavarian countryside acting out my delusions by stringing up debauched clerics and those belonging to the so-called hostile faiths, but have been able to sublimate the evolutionary inanities through art. I am happy to report that <em>The Children of Men</em> does at times rise to that exalted level. <br /><br />Here is a world in which men have gone sterile. You just can't find fertile semen anymore. Some women, denied their customary reproductive roles, have gone bonkers. They end up baptizing cats and dolls and such. (Other women, one imagines, are dancing a jig so tickled are they to never again have to risk another perineum tear.) One thing I liked was the image of the world preparing to go on without mankind. For in the vacuum left by the end of human fertility all the other flora and fauna seem to redouble their efforts. <br /><br />Our hero is Theodore Faron. A sardonic at times bitter retired professor of history at Oxford--there are no more children to teach--who ran his daughter over in a tragic accident many years ago. His wife never forgave him, then she started banging this rugby player half her age. Theo happens to be cousin to the Warden of England, Xan Lyppiatt, a childhood friend, who is running a thuggish police state. During the first half of the story the state is in the process of redistributing its thinning population to central locations for purposes of making delivery of services easier. At least that's the excuse. The first half is all clandestine meetings of the dissidents and background into Theo's boyhood relationship to Xan. <br /><br />Then it turns into a road story not without parallels, though fleeting, to Cormac McCarthy's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Road" title="The Road" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a>. Though, it should be emphasized, this is not a post-apocalyptic world going-out-with-a-bang novel, like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Road" title="The Road" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road</a>, but rather a civilization fizzling-out-with-a-whimper story. Nevertheless there is sufficient violence and craziness and survivalist mentalities employed to keep everyone happy. There is an intimation of the second coming, personal betrayal of the basest sort, and headlong hysterical flight. There is an elegant density of diction that is consistent throughout, and I found that the descriptive sections, especially in the action-packed second half of the book, touch on the beautiful. Highly recommended for thriller lovers. Mandatory for lovers of dystopic fictions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12751088809374851857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1834840350" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1834840350" />
    <a title="Althea Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann"><img alt="Althea Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411566466p2/3846743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1834840350?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Althea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Althea Ann" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann">Althea Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9505694-the-children-of-men">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1834840350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8094850860273920479">Re-read for post-apocalyptic book club.<br/><br/>I liked this book better, the second time around. I read this the first time quite a while ago, and I think perhaps my age has something to do with the difference in perceptions. It's certainly a piece geared toward older readers. Although it contains violence and tension, it's slow-moving, with a quiet, elegiac feel.<br/><br/>Our narrator, Theo, a lonely academic, is the cousin of the Warden of England. The upheaval of the world's current situation has allowed the W</span>
  <span id="freeText8094850860273920479" style="display:none">Re-read for post-apocalyptic book club.<br /><br />I liked this book better, the second time around. I read this the first time quite a while ago, and I think perhaps my age has something to do with the difference in perceptions. It's certainly a piece geared toward older readers. Although it contains violence and tension, it's slow-moving, with a quiet, elegiac feel.<br /><br />Our narrator, Theo, a lonely academic, is the cousin of the Warden of England. The upheaval of the world's current situation has allowed the Warden, Xan, to seize absolute power. The "current situation" is that no children have been born for over two decades, and no others are expected to be born. Humanity is facing its end. <br /><br />However, largely, life goes on as per usual, although with fading hope and increasing ennui. Most citizens, concerned with their daily comfort, do not perceive the iron fist concealed within the Warden's velvet glove. Even Theo doesn't see the Warden as any more than the acquaintance whom he used to spend school holidays with, when they were both boys.<br /><br />But then, Theo is contacted by a tiny group of dissidents with a list of grievances they'd like him to bring to his cousin's attention. And gradually, they win him over to their point of view. (It doesn't hurt that one of them is an intriguingly attractive woman.) The likelihood is that Theo's sympathies won't make any difference. The Warden is in love with power, and not amenable to making any significant changes.<br /><br />But then, a shocking revelation is made: the intriguing dissident is pregnant.<br /><br />Where the book takes it from here is into a complex and insightful exploration of human dynamics. It's full of religious allegory, but certainly does not demand that the reader have 'faith' in order to appreciate its depiction of how religious people might behave in the given situation. (They do a lot of dumb and illogical things within the course of this book, but I found it all utterly believable.) And it's more than that: it's about how we see people vs. how they are, about love, loyalty and betrayal, about guilt and redemption, and of course, the seductive nature of power and the erosion of ideals. <br /><br />I recently read 'The Book of the Unnamed Midwife' and thought that it reminded me of this book (after all, how many post-apocalyptic midwives in a world affected by universal sterility are there in fiction?) but upon re-reading, it's more divergent than I recalled (perhaps the movie version, which is quite different, was beclouding my memories of the book.)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8094850860273920479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/586608422?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586608422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1382236693661588750">This novel seriously freaked me out when I read it. I actually sat in stunned and depressed contemplation at my own lack of children and the decisions I believed I held dear at the time. <br/><br/>I didn't care to bring children into this world, and at the time, I hated the world pretty much entirely, so I got struck against the back of my head after reading this and I haven't really been the same, since.<br/><br/>The novel took me on a very disturbing ride with the ultimate death of humanity by way of sterility. T</span>
  <span id="freeText1382236693661588750" style="display:none">This novel seriously freaked me out when I read it. I actually sat in stunned and depressed contemplation at my own lack of children and the decisions I believed I held dear at the time. <br /><br />I didn't care to bring children into this world, and at the time, I hated the world pretty much entirely, so I got struck against the back of my head after reading this and I haven't really been the same, since.<br /><br />The novel took me on a very disturbing ride with the ultimate death of humanity by way of sterility. The most powerful aspect of the novel was the people's reactions, how their worldviews veered off in strange ways.<br /><br />Suicides were all very well and obvious, but I think I enjoyed the other paths the mind took in reaction.<br /><br />I still can't believe that the novel had the effect of changing my mind about my life. I like to consider myself pretty well-read and aware, but sometimes a huge kick in the head can come out of nowhere. I changed my mind. I wanted to live. I wanted children. I hadn't wanted children before.<br /><br />Very big life choice, no? Maybe it says more about me than the novel. I don't really know. It did surprise the hell out of me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1382236693661588750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1983905260" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1983905260" />
    <a title="Lauren (Shakespeare &amp; Whisky)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32342930-lauren-shakespeare-whisky"><img alt="Lauren (Shakespeare &amp; Whisky)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490838820p2/32342930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1983905260?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren (Shakespeare &amp; Whisky)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren (Shakespeare &amp; Whisky)" href="/user/show/32342930-lauren-shakespeare-whisky">Lauren (Shakespeare &amp; Whisky)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32342930-lauren-shakespeare-whisky?shelf=1-and-2-star-books">1-and-2-star-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32342930-lauren-shakespeare-whisky?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1983905260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14426016462222756877"><i>
  <b>"Early this morning, 1 January 2021, three minutes after midnight, the last human being to be born on earth was killed in a pub brawl in a suburb of Buenos Aires, aged twenty-five years, two months and twelve days."</b>
</i><br/><br/>Despite a riveting premise, I did not enjoy this novel at all.<br/><br/><b>Children of Men</b> struggled to engage me due to an opening act that lasted for the entirety of book 1 ("The Omega"), an unlikeable protagonist and confused thematic messaging. <br/><br/><b>THE PLOT</b><br/><br/><i>
  <b>"We are outraged and demoralized l</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText14426016462222756877" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>"Early this morning, 1 January 2021, three minutes after midnight, the last human being to be born on earth was killed in a pub brawl in a suburb of Buenos Aires, aged twenty-five years, two months and twelve days."</b>
</i><br /><br />Despite a riveting premise, I did not enjoy this novel at all.<br /><br /><b>Children of Men</b> struggled to engage me due to an opening act that lasted for the entirety of book 1 ("The Omega"), an unlikeable protagonist and confused thematic messaging. <br /><br /><b>THE PLOT</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"We are outraged and demoralized less by the impending end of our species, less even by our inability to prevent it, than by our failure to discover the cause."</b>
</i><br /><br />The novel is painfully plodding. Julian, the miracle mother, is not introduced until chapter 6 and the actual action of the novel doesn't occur until past the halfway point. The failure of the novel to properly identify the central plot question results in meandering and self indulgent story telling. <br /><br />On the periphery of a boring story about a privileged, emotionally dead, intellectual, interesting plot lines are suggested. The harrowing forced suicides of the elderly, exploitive immigration policies, total social ennui. <br /><br />But these more interesting tangents are buried beneath a <i>punishingly dull tale</i> that eventually reveals itself to be a confused reflection on the corrupting influence of power and, bizarrely, a religious parable. <br /><br /><b>THE WORLD BUILDING</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"You desire the end but close your eyes to the means. You want the garden to be beautiful, provided that the smell of manure is kept well away from your fastidious nose.”</b>
</i><br /><br />The world building is by far the strongest aspect of <b>Children of Men</b>. The detailed inclusion of geopolitical, psychological and economic impacts of such a cataclysmic event are well- thought out. A number of more recent dystopian writers could do worse than to study James' sophisticated approach. <br /><br />Unfortunately, like so much else in this beleaguered novel, the world building is under utilised. Broad swaths of the world building could have been cut out without any impact on the central plot.<br /><br /><b>THE WRITING</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"If from infancy you treat children as gods they are liable in adulthood to act as devils."</b>
</i><br /><br />The prose was very good. Occasionally even poetic. But so many horrifying scenes were written with a cold detachment that left me distanced from the emotion of the moment. <br /><br /><b>CHARACTERS</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"I don't want anyone to look to me, not for protection, not for happiness, not for love, not for anything.... I have never known what it is to love. I can write those words, know them to be true, but feel only the regret that a tone-deaf man must feel because he can't appreicate music, a regret less keen because it is for something never known, not for something lost.” </b>
</i><br /><br />Despite having finished the book just 40 minutes ago when I got to this section for a moment I couldn't remember Theo's name (the protagonist). He was thoroughly unlikeable. I cannot understand why he was the character chosen as the protagonist. Why, in a story about infertility and a miracle birth, do you position a stuffy, unfriendly man who accidentally killed his only offspring, as the central protagonist?<br /><br />Furthermore, almost all of the side characters were unpleasant. Julian was an insipid, walking womb and mouth piece for "true faith". Xan was a power drunk, closeted homosexual. Nobody in this novel inspired admiration or affection.<br /><br /><b>THEMES</b><br /><br /><i>The Purpose of Life</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"Man is diminished if he lives without knowledge of his past; without hope of a future he becomes a beast.”</b>
</i><br /><br />In her personal life James is a deeply conservative Christian. So it is unsurprising that <b>Children of Men</b>, often described as her best work, explores the premise from a slanted lense. Much effort is expended to compare commercialised, "end of days" religions with Theo and Julian's authentic, private beliefs.<br /><br />The novel is at times scathing of many modern practices; worship of the body, science as god... but James muddies her message by trying to communicate a message by subsuming it beneath the power struggle between two men. <br /><br />She would have done better to write a more open religious parable. It might not have been to my taste but it would have felt coherent. <br /><br /><i>A Meditation on Power</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"A regime which combines perpetual surveillance with total indulgence is hardly conducive to healthy development.”</b>
</i><br /><br />Upon finishing the novel I was left with a sense of squeamish disquiet regarding Theo's triumph over Xan. Theo had overcome spiritual inertia and successfully protected Julian and her son, the saviour of humanity. <br /><br />However, rather then end the novel on this hopeful note James has Theo take control of the country and murder his cousin. This outcome is meant to be ambivalent and I can't understand why it was included. It felt like <b>Children of Men</b> was telling two oxymoronic stories. <br /><br /><i>Spiritual, Emotional and Physical Fertility</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>"Don’t romanticize her. She may be the most important woman in the world but she isn’t the Virgin Mary. The child she is carrying is still the child of a whore.”</b>
</i><br /><br />In a novel that centred on fertility, James managed to make every female character ridiculous. Whether she was describing the deranged women who christened kittens and nurture dolls, repulsive senile elderly women, psychopathic, ugly female leaders or the devout but brainless Julian... they all felt like paper thin shells. <br /><br /><b>Devoid of real characterisation and agency.</b><br /><br />Much has been made of the fact that it is defunct sperm that causes the fertility crisis, as if on this fact alone, the novel should be considered a feminist win. That assertion is frankly ridiculous. <br /><br />I'm baffled by the decision to frame a story of fertility around the memoirs of a privileged male academic who dislikes children, remembers his own daughter with jealousy and guilt, and feels he has suffered no personal loss due to the fertility crisis. <br /><br />It isn't that it can't be done, or that it offends my political principles, it is just that it <i>strips so much potential depth and meaning from the story.</i><br /><br />Overall, I regret reading it and wouldn't recommend it, although readers who enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7082.Do_Androids_Dream_of_Electric_Sheep_" title="Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep? by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38447.The_Handmaid_s_Tale" title="The Handmaid's Tale by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">The Handmaid's Tale</a> (both excellent books with similar themes) may find something to enjoy here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14426016462222756877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1360768552" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360768552" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360768552?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360768552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17994983919458017215">Ugh! I don't like the cover of this book (the one showing on this page). Don't get me wrong, I like Clive Owen, and the 2006 movie is not too shabby but it does not have much to do with the original text apart from the basic premise; and Theo the protagonist of the movie is the polar opposite of the novel’s character. The author P.D. James is best known for her crime fiction novels mostly featuring defective detective Adam Dalgliesh who is also a poet. I have only read a couple of these Dalglies</span>
  <span id="freeText17994983919458017215" style="display:none">Ugh! I don't like the cover of this book (the one showing on this page). Don't get me wrong, I like Clive Owen, and the 2006 movie is not too shabby but it does not have much to do with the original text apart from the basic premise; and Theo the protagonist of the movie is the polar opposite of the novel’s character. The author P.D. James is best known for her crime fiction novels mostly featuring defective detective Adam Dalgliesh who is also a poet. I have only read a couple of these Dalgliesh books and never really cared for them. A “poet-detective” just seems too pretentious and unappealing to me. When I heard that they were filming <i>Children of Men</i> I was intrigued though, I did not expect Ms. James to write a science fiction book worth filming, I thought she was one of those mainstream authors who just want to take a stab at sci-fi without really understanding the genre. Anyway, I first read <i>Children of Men</i> in 2006 shortly before the movie was released because I prefer to read the original source material before watching the movie. I owe P.D. James an apology, she did a stupendous job. That said this book is more “speculative fiction” than sci-fi because there is very little science in it. It is more of a thought experiment where the author explores the social any individual implications of the basic premise, the sort of thing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/874602.Ursula_K__Le_Guin" title="Ursula K. Le Guin" rel="nofollow noopener"> Ursula K. Le Guin</a> excels in.<br /><br /><i>Children of Men</i> can reasonably be labeled as a cozy apocalypse or even a cozy dystopia. It has a high concept premise where in the year 1995* women all over the world suddenly became infertile. As extinction events go this is a very polite one, but quite alarming when you consider the implication. Imagine the human race slowly winding down with a global aging and declining population. In the UK where the novel is set this leads to general despair and ennui in the middle aged and older age groups and uncontrollable wildness in the youngest generation. The year 1995 is called Omega, and the people born in 1995 are called “Omegas”. These Omegas are generally wild and literally allowed to get away with murder because they may be the last hope for mankind's continuation. <br /><br />The event of the novel itself takes place in 2021, 26 years after the year of Omega. The protagonist is called Theo Faron, a disillusioned English gentleman who happens to be related to the Warden of England, a position of supreme power, far in excess of the office of Prime Minister or the President. He used to be a close adviser to the Warden until the day he up and left because he could not stomach the abuses of power. At the beginning of the novel he basically spends all his time just pottering around, not needing to do any work. One day he is approached by a girl called Julian who asks him to contact and petition the Warden about various woes of the British society and the outrageous abuses of power. The petition goes badly leading to the birth of a less than competent group of dissidents. Initially the Warden views these dissidents as something of a joke but soon something momentous happens which causes Theo, Julian and her dissident friends to go on the run. <br /><br />The England P.D. James depicts in this book is a lonely, depressing place where suicide is common, and even encouraged and facilitated by the government. I won't reveal the plot beyond the basic outline already mentioned so far, I do find the book to be very nicely plotted, melancholy, eventually thrilling and the denouement is more than satisfactory. The prose is exquisitely written and makes me want to pick up some more of those Adam Dalgliesh novels just to read more of her beautifully crafted sentences. The main characters are very well drawn, particularly Theo who is very flawed, sympathetic and believable, someone you can really root for. He starts off as a kind of wishy-washy anti-hero:<br /><br /><i>“I don't want anyone to look to me, not for protection, not for happiness, not for love, not for anything.”</i><br /><br />I like how his character gradually transforms by his circumstances as the story progresses. The character of Theo is the polar opposite of the character of the same name portrayed by Clive Owen in the movie version. P.D. James’ Theo is a very polite middle aged and middle class English gentleman, kicking ass and taking names is not in his purview, he is rather awkward and bumbling at times though when push comes to shove he does whatever he has to do.<br /><br />The dialog is also praise worthy with characters getting burned left and right. The switches between the first person epistolary narrative format and the third person narrative seems a little pointless as the narrative point of view is always restricted to Theo and follows the same linear timeline. Still, I am sure James has her artistic reasons and these switches do not impede the readability of the book at all. <br /><br /><i>Children of Men</i> is one of my favorite dystopian books alongside <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984" title="1984 by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">1984</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5479.Brave_New_World___Brave_New_World_Revisited" title="Brave New World / Brave New World Revisited by Aldous Huxley" rel="nofollow noopener"> Brave New World</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/473850.Make_Room__Make_Room_" title="Make Room! Make Room! by Harry Harrison" rel="nofollow noopener"> Make Room! Make Room!</a> etc. This sub genre continues to be very popular today, though the modern dystopian novels tend to be teen adventures for some reason. <i>Children of Men</i> is the real McCoy.<br />_________________<br /><br />* In my <a href="https://www.reddit.com/r/printSF" rel="nofollow noopener">PrintSF</a> sci-fi discussion group I often see someone comment that they don’t want to read “old sci-fi” where the author got their prediction wrong and the future setting of the novel is now the past and these old books are not worth reading because the author was so far off the mark. Well, excuuuuuse me! It is not the job of sci-fi authors to predict the future, the whole point is to speculate and explore the implications. <i>Children of Men</i> is a case in point, P.D. James certainly was not anticipating global infertility to occur 1995 (the book was first published in 1992). This novel – like many great sf novels – is asking “what if”. I shouldn’t mind really, it’s their loss missing out on so many great books but it’s a bee in my bonnet you know.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17994983919458017215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17051186194855061667" style="display:none">Post Apocalyptic, Dystopian, Depressing.<br /><br />It describes a hopeless, sterile future.<br />No more children, no more light, no more expectations.<br /><br />But what if there is a ray of light? <div id="review_905738365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/905738365" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/905738365?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679418733" href="/book/show/77223.The_Children_of_Men">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=up-at-the-crack-of-doom">up-at-the-crack-of-doom</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer905738365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10732119991663298423">It's been more than a quarter century since a human baby was born on earth. Since that time, the aging population has been just sort of hanging around, preparing itself for the inevitable extinction. Some people develop strong attachments to pets or dolls. Others concentrate on self-improvement with adult education classes. BUT, the secretive and rather sinister council keeps a firm grip on everything, regulating the lives and even the deaths of all citizens.<br/><br/>James tells her tale with third perso</span>
  <span id="freeText10732119991663298423" style="display:none">It's been more than a quarter century since a human baby was born on earth. Since that time, the aging population has been just sort of hanging around, preparing itself for the inevitable extinction. Some people develop strong attachments to pets or dolls. Others concentrate on self-improvement with adult education classes. BUT, the secretive and rather sinister council keeps a firm grip on everything, regulating the lives and even the deaths of all citizens.<br /><br />James tells her tale with third person narration and the use of her main character's journal entries. Though a little jarring at first, it turns out to be an effective mix. Her Theo Faron is a history professor, as staid and stodgy a man as you'll ever meet. It's time somebody changed that stick up his ass and a small group of rebels is just the thing to do it. They approach Theo and ask for his help in essentially overthrowing the council. At first he is reluctant, but then...<b>A MIRACLE OCCURS!!!</b> Suddenly, Theo is lifted out of his humdrum life, and, I swear, actually enjoying himself being on the run and in constant danger. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[How very, very sad that the big <b>MIRACLE</b> that should bring hope to all mankind will in all likelihood lead to more horrific regulations from the all-seeing council. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The author earned my admiration for deftly using a fairly unlikable character as her protagonist AND for coming up with the concept of having the end of the world come not in the form of a fiery violent collision with an asteroid, OR a terrible plague (Calling all zombies!), BUT with the sounds of creaking rocking chairs, squeaky walkers and carping bursitis complaints as earth becomes a world full of cranky old people. <br /><br />Genius! </span>
  <a data-text-id="10732119991663298423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3142246646" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3142246646" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38999260-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446483502p2/38999260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3142246646?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/38999260-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571342211" href="/book/show/35718747-the-children-of-men">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3142246646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5870508825659558192">This was another novel on the list of movie adaptations that I really enjoyed and wanted to see how the original source material compared.<br/>Admittedly it’s been so long since I’ve seen the 2006 film but remember it enough to know that the premise are the same but each has a completely different feel.<br/><br/>The main crux is the human population can no longer reproduce, with the last child born 25 years previous and how it impacts on society.<br/><br/>With the story set during 2021 it couldn’t have been more timely,</span>
  <span id="freeText5870508825659558192" style="display:none">This was another novel on the list of movie adaptations that I really enjoyed and wanted to see how the original source material compared.<br />Admittedly it’s been so long since I’ve seen the 2006 film but remember it enough to know that the premise are the same but each has a completely different feel.<br /><br />The main crux is the human population can no longer reproduce, with the last child born 25 years previous and how it impacts on society.<br /><br />With the story set during 2021 it couldn’t have been more timely, I definitely feel that there’s a disconnect with Baby Boomers and Millennials today - so a novel that explores themes of an ageing population without a new up and coming group just highlighted how the young and old complement and need each other.<br /><br />From a maternal instinct of those doting on pets and the need for sex having been diminished to the elderly becoming more of a hindrance to a declining work force, James vividly creates a dysfunctional landscape perfectly.<br /><br />The book is certainly a lot slower paced which actually aided the story that James is trying to tell. <br />The gradual decent into chaos as people lose focuses as inevitable but distant end is more clearly felt.<br /><br />It’s one of them great occurrences where both the book and movie can be enjoyed separately.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5870508825659558192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_813685298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/813685298" />
    <a title="Nigel Mitchell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6021650-nigel-mitchell"><img alt="Nigel Mitchell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442601475p2/6021650.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/813685298?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigel Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigel Mitchell" href="/user/show/6021650-nigel-mitchell">Nigel Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer813685298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3913813107228707040">I read this novel after I saw the movie, and discovered this novel is one of the rare exceptions where the movie is better than the novel. It's not that it was badly written. It's just that the author had the wrong focus.<br/><br/>The novel is set in a near future where humanity has lost the ability to have children. Worldwide sterility has persisted for so long that an entire generation has grown up without any children at all. England has become a dictatorship ruled by Xan Lyppiatt. The main character i</span>
  <span id="freeText3913813107228707040" style="display:none">I read this novel after I saw the movie, and discovered this novel is one of the rare exceptions where the movie is better than the novel. It's not that it was badly written. It's just that the author had the wrong focus.<br /><br />The novel is set in a near future where humanity has lost the ability to have children. Worldwide sterility has persisted for so long that an entire generation has grown up without any children at all. England has become a dictatorship ruled by Xan Lyppiatt. The main character is Dr. Theodore "Theo" Faron, who becomes embroiled in a conflict between a dissident group called the Five Fishes and Xan, his childhood friend and cousin. Along the way, Theo discovers one of the radicals is pregnant with the first unborn child in decades.<br /><br />I know it's unfair to compare a novel is to a movie, but I thought the movie's focus on the implications of a world without children worked well. The novel spent too much time on the politics of the dystopian England. It seemed like P.D. James became fascinated with how someone becomes a dictator. There's a lengthy account of how Xan grew up and ultimately rose to power, which I didn't care about at all. The novel eventually boils down to a discussion on power and the abuse of power. Meanwhile, the story of a childless world became pushed into the background. Honestly, I think P.D. James could have cut out the sterility angle of the novel, and ended up with the same book.<br /><br />That's not to say it was entirely ignored. The novel does delve deeper into the building of the childless world. One of my favorite passages described how some infertile women had been driven insane, and treated dolls as real children. I wish that part had been in the movie.<br /><br /><i>The Children of Men</i> is a good novel held back from becoming a great novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3913813107228707040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10973345" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10973345" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/713041-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206856694p2/713041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10973345?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/713041-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10973345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10781390942732913499">I was disappointed by the film, finding myself unable to muster sympathy for the characters, but I was intrigued by the basic plot and so ventured out to explore the novel. PD James' original creation follows a plot significantly different compared to that of the movie, but I found it to be no less disappointing. The main character, Theo, was perhaps even less likable, due mostly to his lack of conviction about anything during the first half of the book. I was never able to develop an intense fe</span>
  <span id="freeText10781390942732913499" style="display:none">I was disappointed by the film, finding myself unable to muster sympathy for the characters, but I was intrigued by the basic plot and so ventured out to explore the novel. PD James' original creation follows a plot significantly different compared to that of the movie, but I found it to be no less disappointing. The main character, Theo, was perhaps even less likable, due mostly to his lack of conviction about anything during the first half of the book. I was never able to develop an intense fear of or hatred for the government against which the main characters rebelled; the "Council of England" did seem to ignore a few issues of compromised civil-rights, but for the most part presented fairly logical arguments for their pragmatic approach to governance as the human race aged into its final days. Thus, when the inevitable revelation of human pregnancy was revealed and the protagonists embarked on a quest to evade the government until the baby was born, I was unable to share their feelings of fear and despair, and I cared little when characters died. The book moved quickly, especially the second half, which allowed me to follow its absurd plotline through to its disappointing completion - the story was mostly well-written, save for moments of impending excitement that would be introduced with the sentence, "And then it happened." I commend James for her imagination; the basic premise is indeed quite intriguing. I can't say her execution held my interest, though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10781390942732913499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer2997188790878902791">In a word? Disappointing. For the vast majority of this book the primary character, who is written sometimes in third person, and sometimes in first (we read his diary) is dry, vindictive, judgmental, indeed hopeless. I was convinced. Other than being an Oxford professor (certainly a worthy achievement) he had no other redeeming qualities that I could appreciate. Not a whisper of kindness seemed retrievable; he was a conceited Trump-like man, albeit with a better vocabulary and a real degree.<br/><br/>Whe</span>
  <span id="freeText2997188790878902791" style="display:none">In a word? Disappointing. For the vast majority of this book the primary character, who is written sometimes in third person, and sometimes in first (we read his diary) is dry, vindictive, judgmental, indeed hopeless. I was convinced. Other than being an Oxford professor (certainly a worthy achievement) he had no other redeeming qualities that I could appreciate. Not a whisper of kindness seemed retrievable; he was a conceited Trump-like man, albeit with a better vocabulary and a real degree.<br /><br />When, later, we are to believe he is developing a tenuous humanity, even that is limited to his selfishness. He only “cares” about the woman he lusts after. Classic loser male midlife crisis.<br /><br />The premise is interesting - there has been no functioning sperm for twenty-five years - and some scenes were well done. The street scene with two troubled women and a ceramic doll being strolled along was downright creepy and understandable - but overall I knew the entire plot, including the denouement, by about page 30. I wish I had been wrong; so many more intriguing possibilities exist. <br /><br />And the small allegedly seditious group was ridiculous - they had no reason to be together, no plan or credible common goals. Their actions were risible, useless, their failure self-assured. Having spent decades as an activist, imho - these five seemed the opposite of an affinity group. An entropy group maybe. <br /><br />about his mother:<br /><i>“I didn’t love her but I did steal for her. I learned early ... that there are ways of avoiding, without guilt, the commitments of love.”</i><br /><br />about his failed marriage:<br /><i>“I willed myself into the appropriate feelings without knowing what the appropriate feelings were. I endowed her with qualities she didn’t have and then despised her for not having them. Afterwards I might have learned to love her if I had thought more of her needs and less of my own.”</i><br /><br />Ugh.</span>

<div id="review_149737764" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149737764" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149737764?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780676977691" href="/book/show/676681.The_Children_of_Men">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149737764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4507699751333865636">I saw the film adaptation of P. D. James' dystopian tale on television last night - with Caine and Owen reliably excellent - for the third or fourth time; and it reminded me, yet again, how much I'd enjoyed the novel upon which it was (loosely) based. James is one of those middle-aged female British writers - Ruth Rendell/Barbara Vine is another - who put their seemingly endless supply of interesting, somewhat dark stories to the page with a considerable amount of subtlety and elegance stuffed i</span>
  <span id="freeText4507699751333865636" style="display:none">I saw the film adaptation of P. D. James' dystopian tale on television last night - with Caine and Owen reliably excellent - for the third or fourth time; and it reminded me, yet again, how much I'd enjoyed the novel upon which it was (loosely) based. James is one of those middle-aged female British writers - Ruth Rendell/Barbara Vine is another - who put their seemingly endless supply of interesting, somewhat dark stories to the page with a considerable amount of subtlety and elegance stuffed into what otherwise appears to be simple and clean prose. If the general <i>tone</i> of a United Kingdom under the sort of eschatologically despotic government depicted initially strikes one as being a bit too placid and routine, perhaps, with the end date of the human race so signally visible and neither the atheistic nor religious segment of the populace able to claim aught but defeat from the definitive sign of a God's grace which either never existed in the first place, or has clearly been removed with extreme prejudice, James actually managed to find the pulse of the weary, apathetic torpor that would be engendered, replete with zero tolerance for any (overt) acts of criminality and a keenly felt desire to ensure that the imminent extinction of humanity was accompanied by a great deal of cheap, mindless <i>fun</i>.<br /><br />James' Oxford don Theo exemplifies this voluntary abandonment from the affairs of the world with a nicely portrayed controlled despair, having had ample encouragement to join the autocratic <i>Wardenship</i> of his opportunistic cousin and finding himself no longer welcome when such an influential position would do his band of <i>Fishies</i> the most good. Wild tribes of young people having returned to the untamed lure of the forest; machinations surrounding control of the lone and miraculous pregnancy discovered amidst an island of infertility; the cousin-vs-cousin showdown with a crying baby - the noisome sound of the future awakening from a terrifying final sleep - providing the musical score; James handles it all with a professional and assured aplomb. An excellent way to while away an otherwise useless winter day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4507699751333865636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Aldiss <br />416390<br />Paul Bryant's review Jul 10, 2016<br /><b>* * * </b> it was ok<br />bookshelves: sf-novels-aaargh <br /><br />A quote from <i>The Twinkling of an Eye</i>, Brian Aldiss' autobiography:<br /><br />P D James, ordinarily a bestselling middle-class thriller writer, set <i>The Children of Men</i> in the future. The novel was published in 1992. I began to worry about her novel when readers wrote to me, pointing out many similarities between James' novel and my own <i>Greybeard</i>. <i>Greybeard</i> was published by the same publisher, edited by the same editor as James', 30 years earlier; it was still in print... Both centre around Oxford and are set in a world dominated by a tinpot dictator, where there are no more children... <br /><br /><br />******* more at link - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1693950287?book_show_action=true">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> </blockquote><br /><br />I purchased <i>Greybeard</i> on Kindle in Jan of 2018. I'm reading <i>Children of Men</i> in Jan/Feb of 2019. Hopefully I can find time to get back and read this book (<i>Greybeard</i>) sometime later in Feb or March of this year {2019). Maybe check out the movie <i>Children of Men</i>, too !</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15620692569287859990">Loved the movie and can't wait to read the book.<br/><br/>Well, the book and the movie are definitely two separate entities. They even have different endings. P.D. James' book lacks the action and excitement of the film version and P.D. James does go on about things like the decor of Theo's house and the political makeup of her futuristic England. And I would have liked the main character Theo to behave a bit more honorably. But I enjoyed the rendering of a world in which the last baby was born 25 years a</span>
  <span id="freeText15620692569287859990" style="display:none">Loved the movie and can't wait to read the book.<br /><br />Well, the book and the movie are definitely two separate entities. They even have different endings. P.D. James' book lacks the action and excitement of the film version and P.D. James does go on about things like the decor of Theo's house and the political makeup of her futuristic England. And I would have liked the main character Theo to behave a bit more honorably. But I enjoyed the rendering of a world in which the last baby was born 25 years ago.<br /><br />The book did hit home for me as the last of the Omega generation was born about the same time as my 12 year old daughter. I found myself imagining what it would be like bringing up the last of the race, and the heartbreak of knowing that with every milestone my daughter reached, that would be the last time ever. After her, there would be no more.<br /><br />The most interesting part of the book was the description of how the human population dealt with global infertility. Women who would never have children start pushing dolls around in prams as if they were babies or having their cats christened. <br /><br />When I finished, it was a relief to look around and see my neighbor's children playing outside. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15620692569287859990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

The "science" is never really explained, so futuristic rather than sci fi? The film is very different, and not in a good way; fortunately I read the book first.<br/> <br/></span>

<div id="review_1665021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1665021" />
    <a title="Trin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113980-trin"><img alt="Trin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181029461p2/113980.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1665021?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trin" href="/user/show/113980-trin">Trin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446364621" href="/book/show/341423.The_Children_of_Men">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=english-lit">english-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1665021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17007544689429249025">I loved the recent film version of this (which should have gotten WAY more Oscar nominations, dammit!), so of course I had to read the book, which I’d been told was very different. Is it ever! While the basic premise remains the same, many of the events—and pretty much the entire meaning of the novel—were altered for the film. While the movie is LOUD and VIOLENT, the book is quiet and desolate and lonely. The book explores themes of guilt and how men (er, mostly I mean <i>humans</i> here rather than ma</span>
  <span id="freeText17007544689429249025" style="display:none">I loved the recent film version of this (which should have gotten WAY more Oscar nominations, dammit!), so of course I had to read the book, which I’d been told was very different. Is it ever! While the basic premise remains the same, many of the events—and pretty much the entire meaning of the novel—were altered for the film. While the movie is LOUD and VIOLENT, the book is quiet and desolate and lonely. The book explores themes of guilt and how men (er, mostly I mean <i>humans</i> here rather than males, although all the examples given in the text are male) abuse power; the film is about <i>governmental</i> abuse of power far more than individual abuse, and about post-apocalyptic violent desperation rather than quiet despair. It’s interesting, in light of the recent debate about the film adaptation of <i>300</i>; one of the issues raised there is, Can an adaptation contain meanings not present in the original text? Watching <i>Children of Men</i> and then reading the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17452454.P_D__James" title="P.D. James" rel="nofollow noopener"> P.D. James</a> novel provides <i>loads</i> of evidence that the answer is <i>yes</i>. The novel was written in 1992 and expresses, along with universal concerns, others which are specific to its time. (After the superficial ‘greed is good’ ‘80s, have men and women stopped knowing how to love each other?) The film, made in 2006, is about things James couldn’t have dreamed of in 1992; it’s definitely an allegory for our time (as the truly frightening visual allusions to Abu Ghraib towards the end of the film make all-too-clear).<br /><br />Is one better than the other? I felt the film more strongly, possibly because it is so timely. But the book is incredible in its own right, chilling in different but no less effective ways. I’ll be thinking about both for a long, long time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17007544689429249025" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3505028338" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3505028338" />
    <a title="Cphe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe"><img alt="Cphe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378006896p2/14907696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3505028338?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer9359161581799195112">Mankind is dying out and there is no hope for the future. Dystopian novels aren't my usual reading fare but the premise looked interesting and this novel is featured on the Guardian 1000 list.<br/><br/>Found the main character Theodore Faron to be a bit of a cold emotionless fish to begin with. It wasn't until the second half of the novel that he began to come into his own. Wouldn't really class this as a mystery as such although to my mind the story raised more questions than were answered.</span>
